Ham didn’t argue. The shower was a stainless steel cubicle. The water was scalding. He stood under it, letting it beat on his neck and shoulders, washing the mud and the cold sweat away. The adrenaline was leaching out of him, leaving a profound, hollow fatigue. When he emerged, wrapped in a rough towel, Tam had laid out a pair of borrowed pyjamas and a blanket on the sofa.
“Your clothes go in the bag by the door. I’ll incinerate them in the morning. They’ll have trackers in the seams.”
Ham dressed in the pyjamas. They were too short in the leg and arm. He felt ridiculous, and somehow more vulnerable than he had in the woods. He sat on the sofa. Tam was at his computer desk, the dust sheet removed from one monitor. He was typing, his fingers a silent blur.
“The cleaner is visiting my cottage tonight,” Ham said.
Tam didn’t look up. “Good. Expensive, but good. She’ll find what’s there. She won’t find who put it there. That’s a different invoice.”
“I need to know who.”
“You need to sleep,” Tam corrected. “Your brain is soup. My systems are scanning. If your grey van or your harvestman have any digital spoor, I’ll find it. Sleep. That’s the job now.”

Ham lay down on the sofa. The blanket was scratchy wool. He stared at the exposed beams of the ceiling. The fear was still there, a cold stone in his gut, but it was compartmentalised. Tam’s ordered, paranoid world provided a temporary bulwark. The cottage was a fortress. For a few hours, he could stop running.

But as he closed his eyes, he did not see the beams. He saw the ledger from Dundee. The bad numbers. They were not just a mistake. They were a message, written in a language of false projections and hidden clauses. A message that said: We own the reality you think you inhabit. We can change its physics.

And he saw the hand flicking ash from the van. Pale, long-fingered. An executive’s hand. Not a thug’s. This was the new violence. It didn’t break down your door. It changed the numbers on your mortgage. It sent a quiet van and a man with a telescopic lens. It used the system itself as the bludgeon.

He had thought the hook was in his gut. He was wrong. The hook was in the world around him. It was in every contract, every signal, every digital whisper. They weren’t trying to reel him in. They were trying to turn the very water he swam in to poison.

He slept, but it was a thin, shallow sleep. He dreamed of his father’s granite headstone in the fog. The dates were wrong. They kept changing.

He was wrenched awake by a sound. Not a loud sound. A soft, rhythmic pinging.

He opened his eyes. The cottage was dark but for the glow of Tam’s main monitor. Tam was standing before it, rigid, his small frame silhouetted. On the screen was a satellite map. A red dot pulsed rhythmically, emitting the soft ping he had heard.

“What is it?” Ham’s voice was gravel.
Tam didn’t turn. “The cleaner finished at your cottage ninety minutes ago. She planted a secondary device. A tracker. On the principle that whoever comes to check her work is more important than the work itself.”
Ham stood, walking to the desk. The map was of his village. The red dot was moving, slowly, down the single-track road that led to the coast. To the lighthouse.
“They found the static was removed,” Tam said, his voice flat. “And they’re not happy. They’re following the only lead they have.”
“What lead?”
Tam zoomed the map. A second, fainter dot appeared, trailing the first by half a mile. A data tag beside it read: SIGINT – MOBILE INTERCEPT – HIGHLANDER01.
“They’ve cloned your burner phone,” Tam said. “The signal is faint, proxy-routed, but they have a general direction. They’re following the cleaner’s tracker, but they’re also homing in on your last active signal area. They’ll be here inside an hour.”
Ham stared at the two dots, crawling across the screen like blood cells in a capillary. The hammer and the anvil. And he was in between.
“You said this place was defended.”
“It is,” Tam said. “Against burglars. Against curious police. Not against a professional, funded incursion. This…” He gestured at the screen. “This is an audit. And they mean to balance the books.”
He turned to look at Ham, his face grim in the monitor’s light. “The sofa is revoked. You need to be gone before they get here. And I need to burn this place to the ground.”
“Tam, I—”
“Don’t,” Tam snapped. “This is the cost of doing business with a marked man. I knew the risk when I opened the door. Now, you have fifty minutes. There’s a cave at the base of the cliffs, east of the point. The entrance is underwater at high tide. It’s low tide now. You can get in. There’s a chamber above the waterline. Supplies. Wait for two full tidal cycles. Then go.”
He went to a cupboard, pulled out a heavy-duty plastic dry bag. “Clothes. Water. Food. A different phone. Don’t turn it on unless you see the sun rise twice.”
Ham took the bag. “I’ll make this right.”
“You’ll make it right by surviving,” Tam said, already pulling cables from his computers. “Now, go. Out the back. Follow the cliff path east for four hundred yards. You’ll see a rope ladder. It leads down to a ledge. The cave mouth is to the left. Mind the swell.”
Ham pulled on the borrowed boots Tam thrust at him. He slung the dry bag over his shoulder. At the back door, he paused.
“The numbers in the Dundee ledger,” he said. “Who do they point to?”
Tam didn’t look up from his work of systematic erasure. “They don’t point to a who, Ham. They point to a what. A shell, inside a trust, inside a holding company registered in a jurisdiction that doesn’t exist on most maps. The kind of entity that isn’t owned by people. It’s a tool. A scalpel. And someone very powerful is holding the handle.”
He finally met Ham’s eyes. “You’re not fighting a rival. You’re fighting a symptom. Now go.”

Ham went out into the night. The wind had picked up, howling in off the sea, carrying spits of rain. He found the cliff path, a thin, treacherous line along the edge of the world. Below, the North Sea roared, a black chaos of white foam.

He found the rope ladder, anchored to rusted iron stakes driven deep into the turf. He tested it, then began his descent into the roaring dark. The ladder swayed and twisted. The bag on his back threatened to pull him off. He climbed down, hand under hand, into the gullet of the storm.

He found the ledge. To the left, as promised, a dark arch in the cliff face, just above the seething, sucking water. He timed the swell, and on the retreat of a wave, he leapt across the gap, landing on slick rock inside the mouth. He scrambled up, away from the water, into a narrow tunnel that smelled of salt and decay.

He did not look back. He crawled forward until the tunnel opened into a small, domed chamber. A single, battery-powered lantern sat on a flat rock. He switched it on, a low, blue-white LED glow revealing bottled water, protein bars, a foil blanket. A tomb, or a womb.

He sat on the cold stone, his back against the wall, and listened to the sea gnaw at the cliff outside. The hook was no longer in his gut. It was in the ledger. It was in the shell company. It was in the cloned signal from his phone. It was in the very architecture of the world he had helped build, a world where power was no longer a fist, but an algorithm, a clause, a quiet van on a wet street.

He had thought he was measuring the cut. He had thought he was clearing the site.

But the site was the whole world. And the predators were not in the trees. They were in the boardrooms, their tools not teeth and claws, but lawyers and accountants and men with pale, long-fingered hands.

The ghost’s phone was in the dry bag. He did not reach for it. He sat in the absolute dark, beyond the lantern’s glow, and waited for the tide to rise and seal him in. The first rule of joinery was to measure twice, cut once. He had measured his enemy and found them to be a reflection in a black mirror, formless, vast, and everywhere.

The only cut left was to disappear. To become a ghost himself. But first, he had to wait out the tide. And in the roaring dark, with the water rising, he began to plan not a defence, but a counter-cut. A cut that would not be measured in wood, but in blood and ruin.

---

 Chapter 15

 CHAPTER 15

The tide reached its peak three hours after midnight. The sound was not a roar, but a suffocation. The sea filled the lower tunnel, pressing the air in the domed chamber into a dense, wet blanket. Ham sat in the absolute dark, the blue-white lantern extinguished, and counted the seconds between the cavern’s breath—a deep, guttural inhalation as a wave receded, then a stifled, shuddering exhalation as the next surge compressed the space. The rock wept condensation. The temperature dropped. He did not move.

He was measuring a different cut now. Not of timber or stone, but of time. Of attention spans. Of institutional memory. He was a flaw in the ledger, a ghost in the algorithm. They would be looking for a man running, for a signal pinging, for a withdrawal from an account, for a face on a camera. He would give them nothing but a perfect, patient zero. The most powerful cut was the one that removed a piece so cleanly the structure did not yet know it was gone.

When the sea’s breath began to lengthen, the intervals growing, the pressure easing, he moved. He did not switch on the light. His hands found the dry bag by memory. He unsealed it, his fingers numb and slick with salt damp. Inside was the ghost’s phone, a cheap burner with a prepaid SIM, its battery full. He did not turn it on. Beneath it was a sheaf of papers, laminated against the damp, and a pencil. He felt their edges in the dark.

He waited another hour, until the retreating water echoed with a hollow, dripping finality. Then he sparked the lantern to its lowest setting. The blue glow made a cave of his face. He spread the papers on the flat rock. They were not printouts. They were hand-drawn diagrams, connections in clean, precise architectural lines. A topology of influence. Names were not written, but coded as numbered holdings, as shell companies nested like Russian dolls. He knew them anyway. He had built some of the dolls himself.

At the centre of the web was not a name, but a symbol: a stylised ‘M’ enclosed within a diamond. Mactire Holdings. The wolf. It was a joke, he saw now, a piece of private, predatory humour. The wolf does not concern itself with the opinion of the sheep. The wolf designs the pasture, owns the slaughterhouse, and sells the mutton back to the flock at a premium.

His own work was there. The Dundee site, a proposed logistics hub, was a spoke. The environmental surveys he’d buried, the planning objections he’d smoothed, the compulsory purchase orders he’d orchestrated—they were not just profit. They were alignment. They were ensuring a certain pipeline of goods—goods that would never appear on any customs form—flowed through a certain port, onto certain trucks, into the bloodstream of the country, untraceable. He had thought he was clearing a site for a client. He had been sterilising a needle.

The hook was in the ledger. The ghost, the accountant with his pale, long-fingered hands, had not been stealing. He had been auditing. And he had found the cancer. So they had cut him out.

Ham’s pencil hovered over the diagram. He was not an accountant. He was a joiner. He understood stress, load, leverage. A structure could be brought down not by attacking the main pillar, but by removing a single, precisely chosen keystone. The illusion of integrity would hold for a moment, a breath, before the whole edifice sheared along its hidden fault lines.

He found it. Not Mactire. That was the head of the beast, armoured, distant. He found a joint. A company called ‘Bryce & Sloane, Corporate Services’. A bland, forgettable name. It was the nominal owner of the Dundee site. It was also, according to the ghost’s neat arrows, the administrator for seven of the nested shells. It was the postbox, the secretary, the cleaner. It was the single point of contact, the humble clerk who knew where all the bodies were filed.

He memorised the address in London. A nondescript building on a street of identical nondescript buildings.

The lantern battery began to dim. He repacked the bag, sealing the phone inside its Faraday pouch. He drank tepid water, ate a protein bar, the taste of chalk and false chocolate. He folded the foil blanket into a tight square. He would leave no trace.

As the first grey light of a filthy dawn seeped into the chamber from the tunnel mouth, he moved. The air was cold and sharp with ozone. The rocks were black and glistening. He moved down the tunnel, his body a shadow against the gloom, and peered out. The sea was a sullen, heaving grey, retreating, leaving behind a scum of foam and shredded weed. The cliff face wept. The storm had blown itself out, leaving a bruised, exhausted stillness.

He waited for the rhythm of the waves. On the trough of a large swell, he leapt back across the gap to the wet ledge. The climb up was harder. His muscles were stiff, his mind a crystal of cold purpose. He found the path, a sheep track disguised as erosion, and followed it up and away from the sea, into the low, clinging mist that shrouded the headland.

He emerged not at his car, but two miles north, at a lay-by overlooking a different bay. A different car waited, a ten-year-old Volvo estate, mud-spattered, anonymous. The keys were in a magnetic box under the wheel arch. The ghost had been thorough. Ham opened the boot. Inside was a kit: clean, serviceable clothes from a supermarket; a waterproof coat; a canvas holdall; a wallet with cash, a driver’s licence with his photo and the name ‘Alasdair Grant’; two more burners; and a set of lockpicks in a leather roll.

He stripped where he stood, the wind biting his skin. He bundled his wet, salt-stiff clothes and shoved them deep into a roadside bin already overflowing with tourist detritus. He dressed as Alasdair Grant. The clothes fit. The identity felt like a cheap suit. He got in the car. It started with a dutiful rumble. The fuel tank was full.

He drove south, not towards the bridges and the motorways, but inland, through the glens. He took the slow roads, the roads that coiled through sleeping villages and over empty passes. He was a flaw moving through the system. He needed to think. A counter-cut required more than a target. It required understanding the grain of the material. You did not swing an axe at oak. You used a saw, with fine teeth, and you let the tool do the work.

The tool was information. The ghost had gathered it, but he had not used it. He had been an accountant to the last, believing the truth itself was a weapon. It was not. Truth was raw timber. It needed to be planed, jointed, fitted into a narrative that would bear weight. It needed to be placed where it would cause maximum structural failure.

He stopped at a roadside café, a greasy spoon with steamed-up windows. He ordered a full breakfast and ate every greasy, salty mouthful, feeling the calories burn like coal in a furnace. He paid in cash. He used the toilet, scrubbing the salt from his face and neck. In the mirror, Alasdair Grant looked back: a man in his late forties, weathered, unremarkable, a contractor or a surveyor, someone who belonged on these roads.

Back in the car, he drove until he found a public library in a market town. He parked three streets away. Inside, he found a computer terminal. He used a guest pass. He did not search for Bryce & Sloane. He searched for obituaries.

He found it in the Inverness Courier, three weeks old. ‘Tragic Loss of Promising Young Accountant’. A photograph of a smiling, generic face. The body of Euan MacLeod had been found by hillwalkers on a remote stretch of the Speyside way. Exposure, said the police. A tragic accident. He was a keen amateur naturalist, often went walking alone. The article quoted a grieving fiancée, devastated parents. It was a perfect, quiet closing of a file.

Ham read it twice. The grain was wrong. The ghost—Euan—had not been a hillwalker. He had been a man who feared mud on his polished shoes. He had hated the wind. This was a narrative that had been planed smooth and fitted into place. A counter-narrative would need to be rougher, truer, and driven in with force.

He cleared the browser history. He left the library. The afternoon was fading into a damp, early twilight. He drove east now, towards the coast again, but a different one. He needed a port. Not a major one. A minor one. A place where the rhythm was slower, where eyes were accustomed to the ordinary.

He reached Peterhead as the streetlights flickered on. The harbour was a forest of masts and rigging, trawlers and supply boats. The air smelled of diesel, fish guts, and rust. He found a pub a block back from the docks, the kind with a stained carpet and a silent television showing horse racing. He took a corner table with his back to the wall.

He nursed a pint of heavy, watching the door. Men came in, men in overalls and thick jackets, their faces lined with salt and wind. They spoke in low bursts, about catches, about engines, about the price of fuel. This was a world of tangible things. Of nets and engines and tonnes of fish. It was a world he understood better than the world of shell companies and algorithms. Here, power was a winch, a fist, a bigger boat.

He finished his pint. He went to the payphone in the dim hallway by the toilets. It smelled of stale urine and bleach. He fed it coins. He dialled a number from memory, one he had not dialled in fifteen years. A number that belonged to a world before ledgers, before desks, before the Persian rug.

It rang five times. A voice answered, a voice like gravel shifting in a bucket. “Aye?”

“It’s Ham.”

A long pause. The sound of a cigarette being drawn. “Ham. Christ. You’re a voice from the grave.”

“I need to be, for a while.”

Another pause. “Trouble?”

“The kind that doesn’t wash off.”

“Where are you?”

“North.”

“You’ll be wanting a boat, then.”

“I’ll be wanting a word with a man who knows boats. A man who doesn’t like questions.”

The gravelly voice grunted. “The Morna. Skipper’s name is Tam. He’s at the berth at the far south end. Green hull, white superstructure. He’ll be loading gear for the morning tide. Tell him Archie said you were sound. He won’t ask, but you’ll need cash. More than you think.”

“I have it.”

“Aye. You always did. Don’t call this number again, Ham. It’s not a good time for ghosts.”

The line went dead. Ham replaced the receiver. Archie. A deckhand from a lifetime ago, when Ham had worked the summer on the ferries, before university, before the world of projections and revised figures. A man who knew the value of silence.

He left the pub. The night air was knife-cold. He walked the docks, past crates of nets, pallets of coiled rope. The Morna was where Archie said it would be, a sturdy sixty-foot trawler, its paintwork scarred and honest. A man in oilskins was hauling a crate aboard from the quayside.

“Tam,” Ham said.

The man straightened, a silhouette against the dock light. He was broad, with a beard that was more salt than pepper. He said nothing.

“Archie said I was sound.”

Tam spat into the black water between the boat and the quay. “Archie’s a sentimental bastard. What do you want?”

“Passage. One way. No paperwork.”

“Where to?”

“Norway. Bergen.”

Tam laughed, a short, harsh sound. “This isn’t a fucking cruise liner. We’re fishing. Dogger Bank, then maybe up towards Stavanger if the weather holds.”

“Stavanger would do.”

“It’s a working boat. You work. You stow gear, you bait lines, you clean the catch. You puke over the side, you clean that up too. You’re just another pair of hands. A thousand. Cash. Now.”

Ham counted out ten notes from the wallet of Alasdair Grant. He handed them over. Tam didn’t count them. He stuffed them into the chest pocket of his oilskins.

“Be here at four. We sail with the tide. You’re late, you’re left. You cause trouble, you go over the side. Understood?”

“Understood.”

“Got gear?”

“A bag.”

“Bring it. And boots. It’ll be wet.”

Ham nodded. Tam turned back to his crate, the conversation evidently over. Ham walked back to the Volvo. He drove to a cheap bed-and-breakfast on the outskirts, paid for a night in cash, and lay on a thin bed under a nylon quilt. He did not sleep. He listened to the wind rattle the window frame. He thought of joints and keystones. Bryce & Sloane, Corporate Services. A postbox in London.

He was not going to London. Not yet. London was where they would expect a flaw to go, to rage against the machine. He was going to become water. To flow through the cracks they hadn’t considered, to the source of the pressure. The ghost’s diagrams had shown a flow of capital, but capital needed to be laundered, to be made real. It needed physical anchors. Ships. Ports. Warehouses.

The Morna was not an escape. It was his first cut into the grain of their world. A world that relied on the invisible movement of things. He would start by becoming one of those things, a silent, moving part in the great, grinding machine. From the inside, he would find the rust. From the inside, he would measure the cut.

At half-three, he rose. He packed the holdall. He left the Volvo in the B&B car park, the keys in the ignition. Let it be someone else’s problem. He walked through the sleeping town to the docks, his boots echoing on the wet cobbles. The Morna’s engine was already grumbling, a deep, phlegmy vibration in the pre-dawn dark. A plume of diesel exhaust stained the air.

Tam was on deck, a shadow against the wheelhouse light. He saw Ham and jerked his head towards the stern. Ham stepped aboard. The deck moved under his feet, a familiar, uneasy roll. He stowed his bag in a tiny, fetid cabin that smelled of sweat and old fish. He went back on deck.

Two other men were there, younger, moving with the tired efficiency of men who had done this a thousand times. They ignored him. Tam gestured to a stack of plastic crates. “Bait. Get it sorted. Hooks in the trays. Don’t tangle the lines.”

Ham found a knife and got to work. The bait was frozen mackerel, slick and grey. The hooks were cruel and sharp. His hands, which had once signed million-pound orders, which had poured sixteen-year-old Lagavulin onto a Persian rug, now grew numb with cold and fish slime. The rhythm was simple, physical, mindless. It was a kind of purity.

As the sky lightened to a dirty grey, the Morna cast off its lines. The engine deepened to a full-throated roar. The boat turned its blunt nose towards the harbour mouth, towards the open sea. The town lights receded, became a dim, lonely cluster against the dark land.

Ham stood at the stern, watching the wake churn white against the black water. He was not Alasdair Grant. He was not Hamish MacTavish. He was a pair of hands on a boat in the North Sea, a flaw in the system, moving silently towards the source of the stain. The hook was no longer in his gut. He was the hook. And he was being played out on a long, dark line into deeper, colder water, where the larger predators swam.

He turned his face into the biting wind and spray. The first rule of joinery was to measure twice, cut once. He had taken the measure of his enemy in the roaring dark of a sea cave. Now, with the salt air filling his lungs and the stink of bait on his hands, he began, with infinite care, to sharpen his blade.

---

 Chapter 16

 CHAPTER 16

The bait was gone. The last frozen mackerel, its eye a milky marble, slid from Ham’s numb fingers into the churning green trough of the wake. He watched it vanish, a brief grey flicker before the sea swallowed it. His hands were raw, cross-hatched with fine cuts from the hooks, the skin bloated and pale from hours of salt and ice melt. The physical work was a blunt instrument. It had driven thought from his head, leaving only the animal rhythm of the task: slit, hook, stack. Slit, hook, stack.

Now, with the crates empty and the lines coiled in their tubs like sleeping snakes, the thinking returned. It came not as a flood, but as a slow, cold seep, like seawater finding a hairline crack in a hull.

The Morna was ten miles out. The Scottish coast was a smudge of charcoal on the horizon, the town of Cairnburgh no longer a cluster of lights but a memory. Here, the North Sea showed its true face. It was not the postcard blue of summer brochures, but a sullen, leaden green, heaving under a sky the colour of wet slate. The waves were not white-capped and merry, but long, rolling hills of water that lifted the trawler with a ponderous, indifferent strength before letting it slide into the troughs with a stomach-dropping lurch. The air tasted of salt and diesel and a deeper, older rot.

Tam was a silhouette in the wheelhouse, one hand on the worn teak wheel, his body swaying with the boat’s motion as if he were rooted to the deck. The two deckhands—Donnie and Rabbie, Ham had gleaned—were forward, smoking, their voices carried away on the wind. They treated Ham as furniture, a piece of equipment that had been loaded aboard. It suited him.

He moved to the leeward side, out of the worst bite of the wind, and lit a cigarette. The flame from his lighter was a tiny, defiant eye in the gloom. He drew the smoke deep, felt it burn a familiar path. The action was a relic, a ritual from a previous life. Here, it was just warmth.

His mind worked. Not on the problem of Alasdair Grant, or the ledger of wrongs that stretched from a Persian rug to a sea cave. Those were abstracts now, pictures in a book he had closed. His mind worked on the immediate calculus of the boat. The Morna was a sixty-foot stern trawler, old but cared for. The engine’s vibration spoke of rebuilt cylinders, tight bearings. The deck was scarred but scrubbed clean of fish guts. This was not a boat for joyriding. It was a tool, and Tam was a craftsman. The run to the oil platform, the Cormorant Alpha, was a delivery. A specialised delivery. Ham had seen the crates loaded under tarpaulin in the pre-dawn dark. They were not bait. They were the wrong shape, the wrong weight. They had been handled with a care that frozen fish did not require.

The platform. That was the knot. The Cormorant Alpha was a fixed steel jacket, a veteran of the field, production in gentle decline but still a small city of steel perched on stilts in the sea. A place of shift changes and supply runs, of routine and procedure. And of isolation. A perfect place for a man to disappear. A perfect place for a thing to be lost, or found, or exchanged.

Ham finished the cigarette and flicked the butt over the side. It was swallowed instantly. He was not here to find Alasdair Grant. Grant was a symptom. He was here to find the disease. The platform was not the destination; it was the syringe.

A bell clanged from the wheelhouse. Donnie and Rabbie stubbed out their own cigarettes and began moving with a renewed purpose. Ham watched. They went to a steel hatch set flush in the deck just aft of the wheelhouse. Rabbie bent, heaved it open. A smell rose from the darkness below—not of fish, but of engine oil, stale air, and something else, metallic and sweet. Donnie descended, his boots clanging on an iron ladder. Rabbie followed.

Tam’s voice, amplified by a small loudspeaker, crackled above the wind. “MacTavish. Here.”

Ham went to the wheelhouse door, ducked inside. The space was warm, thick with the smell of old tobacco, boiled coffee, and wet wool. Radar screens glowed green, their sweeps painting the empty sea. A GPS unit chirped softly. Tam’s eyes were fixed on the horizon, his hands making minute adjustments to the wheel.

“You can steer?” Tam asked, not looking at him.

“Aye.”

“Take her. Heading two-seven-zero. Keep her there.”

It was not a request. It was a test. Ham moved to the wheel, his hands replacing Tam’s on the smooth, varnished wood. He felt the live kick of the sea through the spokes, the tremor of the engine through the sole. He adjusted a degree to port, felt the bow dig into the oncoming swell, and corrected. The compass needle settled.

Tam grunted, a sound of neither approval nor disapproval. He poured thick black coffee from a thermos into a chipped enamel mug and handed it to Ham. “Two hours to the Cormorant. You’ll take the first crate up with Donnie. You’re the hook. He’s the line.”

“What’s in the crate?”

“You don’t need to know.”

“If I’m carrying it, I do.”

Tam turned his head, his eyes like chips of flint in the wheelhouse gloom. “It’s a part. A specialised part. For the gas compression system. Documentation is in order. You’re a specialist contractor. Your name is John Gair. You have a pass.”

“And if they check?”

“They won’t. Not beyond the paperwork. It’s a Tuesday. The shift supervisor is a man named Lyle. He likes the football. Donnie knows the score from Sunday. They’ll talk. You’ll stand there and look like a man who knows about compressors. Which you don’t. So you’ll say nothing. Nod. Look bored.”

“And after the delivery?”

“You get back on the boat. We leave.”

“That’s all?”

“That’s the job,” Tam said, his voice final. He took a folded sheet of paper from his pocket and put it on the chart table. A photocopied technical drawing, a badge with a blurred photo and the name John Gair, and a single key on a steel fob. “Memorise the diagram. The key is for the crate. You open it, you show the part to Lyle, you close it. You lock it. You bring the key back.”

Ham kept one hand on the wheel, his eyes on the grey water ahead. He took a sip of the coffee. It was bitter, strong, scalding. “And if Lyle wants to inspect it more closely?”

“He won’t. It’s a sealed unit. Tampered with, the warranty voids. The paperwork says so. He’s a company man. He likes boxes with ticks in them. You’ll give him the box to tick.”

The simplicity of it was its own kind of genius. The boredom of routine was the best camouflage. A man doing an unusual thing stands out. A man doing a boring, paperwork-heavy thing is invisible. Ham was to be part of the paperwork.

For an hour, he steered. The monotony was a meditation. The wheel fought him, a living thing. He learned its language, the subtle pressure of a following sea, the jerk of a cross-swell. He thought of joinery. Of the hidden joint, the one that bore the weight. It was never the most obvious. It was the housed dovetail, the mortise and tenon, glued and clamped and unseen inside the finished piece. This delivery was a housed dovetail. His role was the glue. Unseen, holding the structure together just long enough.

When Rabbie’s head appeared at the wheelhouse door, Ham relinquished the wheel. He went below, into the belly of the boat. The space was cramped, a cave of steel ribs and pipes. The crate sat amidships, strapped to the deck. It was a heavy-gauge plastic transport case, olive green, with pressure-equalisation valves and rubber seals. It bore stencilled markings: FRAGILE – PRECISION CALIBRATION – DO NOT X-RAY. Donnie was attaching a web of nylon straps to it, forming a harness.

“You’ll take the front,” Donnie said, his voice toneless. “Lighter load going up. Heavier coming down. We switch for the return.”

Ham nodded. He studied the crate. It was not large, perhaps three feet by two. But its density was apparent. It sat on the deck with a profound stillness. He touched the cold plastic. The metallic sweet smell was stronger here. It was a smell he knew. It was the smell of high-grade machine oil, and of money.

The Cormorant Alpha appeared first as a prick of light in the murk, then as a faint geometric sketch against the cloud. As the Morna drew closer, the sketch resolved into a monstrosity of scale. The platform was a jagged black cliff of steel rising from the sea, crowned with a labyrinth of pipes, towers, and flaming gas vents. The flare stack burned a pale, heat-shimmering tongue against the grey, a perpetual sacrifice to the gods of pressure and profit. The sound reached them across the water—a deep, industrial hum, the shriek of metal on metal, the hiss of steam. It was the sound of a beast feeding.

Tam brought the Morna in close to the service landing, a steel platform littered with containers and drums, slick with spray. A crane arm swung out from the platform’s side, a hook dangling. Donnie attached the straps. A signal was given. The crane whined, took the weight, and lifted the crate from the deck in a smooth, practised arc. It hung for a moment against the vast grey wall of the platform, a plastic egg against a steel mountain, then was drawn in and out of sight.

A personnel basket was lowered. Ham and Donnie climbed in. It was a cage of welded bars, a floating prison cell. A jerk, and they were hauled up, the Morna shrinking below them to a toy boat in a bathtub. The wind plucked at their clothes. The sea churned, a foaming maw. Up they went, past dripping steel columns crusted with mussels and barnacles, past pipelines big enough to walk through, into the roaring, oily heart of the beast.

The basket clanged onto the deck. A man in orange overalls and a hard hat unlocked the gate. This was Lyle. He had a tired face, a clipboard, and eyes that had seen ten thousand deliveries.

“Gair?” he shouted over the platform’s din.

Ham nodded, tapped his badge.

“And Donnie. Back again. How’d the Jags get on?”

“Robbed. Penalty in the eighty-ninth minute. You see it?”

“Aye. Criminal. Right, this way. Keep to the yellow walkway.”

They followed him through a canyon of machinery. The noise was a physical pressure. Pumps thumped, compressors whined, valves hissed. The air was warm, thick with the smell of hydrocarbons, hot metal, and a faint, eggy trace of hydrogen sulphide. The walkway vibrated underfoot. Every surface was painted in thick, industrial colours—yellow, grey, safety red. It was a world devoid of softness, of nature. It was a pure expression of function, of extracted power.

The crate waited in a small, brightly lit storeroom labelled QUARANTINED INTAKE. Lyle checked the paperwork against the serial number on the crate. He ticked boxes. “Need to verify the unit. Open her up.”

Ham used the key. The seals broke with a soft hiss. The lid opened on heavy hinges. Inside, nestled in custom-cut grey foam, was a cylinder of brushed stainless steel, complex with sensor ports and connector heads. It gleamed under the fluorescent lights. It looked precisely like a gas analyser module. Ham had memorised the diagram. He pointed to a calibration port. “Seal’s intact. You can see the serial here.”

Lyle leaned in, peered, nodded. He didn’t touch it. He ticked another box. “Aye. Good. Shut it. Lock it. It’ll be collected by the systems team at 1800. You’re done.”

Ham closed the lid. The lock clicked shut. He pocketed the key. The transaction was complete. The housed dovetail was glued.

Lyle led them back out towards the landing stage. The walkway passed a large, internal window looking into a control room. Men in clean overalls sat before banks of screens, their faces lit by the glow of schematics and pressure graphs. It was the brain of the beast, quiet, climate-controlled.

Ham’s gaze swept the room, a casual glance. Then it stopped.

At the far end of the room, leaning over the shoulder of a technician, pointing at a screen, was a man in a navy-blue polo shirt and khaki trousers. He had a lean, alert frame, close-cropped grey hair. He was speaking, his mouth moving silently behind the glass. He turned slightly, profile sharp.

It was the man from the charity dinner in Edinburgh. The one who had been with Alasdair Grant. The one with the quiet voice and the watchful eyes that missed nothing. The civil servant. The fixer.

His name came to Ham then, surfacing from the dark ledger of his memory: Malcolm Grierson. Senior policy advisor. A man who moved in the corridors where power was not shouted, but whispered.

Grierson was here. On a declining production platform on a Tuesday, looking at system schematics.

Donnie was already stepping into the personnel basket, his back turned. Lyle was waving them on. Ham forced his legs to move, his face to remain a blank slab of boredom. He stepped into the cage, his mind screaming.

The basket jerked, began its descent. Ham watched the control room window shrink above him. Grierson had turned away, was now speaking into a radio. He had not looked out the window. He had not seen Ham.

The descent was a void. The roaring of the platform faded, replaced by the wind and the sea. The Morna grew larger. Ham’s hands were steady on the cold bars of the cage. Inside, his thoughts were a vortex.

This was not a delivery. It was an insertion. The crate was not the payload. He was. His presence, logged as John Gair, specialist contractor, was the housed dovetail. He had been the glue, facilitating the delivery of the real item—the information, the access, the thing that Grierson was here to collect or to plant. The platform’s systems were the target. The Cormorant Alpha was not a waypoint. It was the point.

The basket hit the deck of the Morna. Tam had the engine already grumbling, ready to cast off. Donnie and Ham unclipped the harness. The crane hook swung away.

“Get the lines,” Tam barked.

Ham moved, his body obeying while his mind raced. He coiled ropes, his eyes scanning the towering wall of the platform. Up there, in that steel hive, Malcolm Grierson was conducting business. The kind of business that required a man like Alasdair Grant to run interference, to create noise and chaos on the shore. The kind of business that required a patsy like Hamish MacTavish to draw attention, to be the obvious, angry man waving a fist at the storm, while the real work was done here, in the sterile, roaring heart of the machine.

The Morna pulled away from the platform, turning its stern to the towering structure. Ham stood at the rail, watching it recede. The flare burned its wasteful, brilliant flame. It was a beacon, but not of safety. It was a signal fire for the corrupt, a lighthouse for the predators who swam in the deep, cold water of state and corporate power.

He had come to find the source of the stain. He had found a pipeline. And he was no longer the hook. He was the bait that had been dangled past the teeth of the beast, to see what would stir in the dark.

Tam came to stand beside him, following his gaze back to the platform. He lit a cigarette, offered the pack to Ham. Ham took one. They smoked in silence as the distance grew.

“Job’s done,” Tam said, finally.

“Is it?” Ham asked, his voice low, lost in the wind and engine noise.

Tam looked at him, a long, assessing look. He said nothing. He drew on his cigarette, then flicked it over the side. “Get below. Stow the gear. We’ve a long steam back.”

Ham went below. The empty space where the crate had been was a void. The sweet, metallic smell lingered. He sat on a low bench, the vibration of the engine travelling up through his bones. He took the key from his pocket. The steel fob was cold. He turned it over in his palm.

He had measured twice. The measurement was wrong. The cut he had planned was insufficient. The enemy was not a single man, or a rogue company. It was a system. A system that used men like Grant as blunt instruments, and men like Grierson as precision tools. A system that could turn a whisky-stained businessman into a fugitive, and a fugitive into an unwitting courier.

The blade he had been sharpening was too small. It was a filleting knife. He needed a axe. He needed to find the root of the tree, not just prune a rotten branch.

The Morna pushed east, away from the setting sun that bled a dull orange through the cloud. The platform became a distant, spiked silhouette, then a mirage, then nothing.

Ham remained below, in the dim, throbbing belly of the boat. The hook was no longer in his gut. It was in his hand. A small, cold key. And he knew, with a certainty that settled in him like ballast, that it did not open a crate. It opened a door. A door he had just delivered to the beast’s own lair. And the only way to close it was to walk through.

---

 Chapter 17

 CHAPTER 17

The Morna docked at a private berth in Peterhead two hours before dawn. The harbour was a graveyard of shadows and sodium light, the air thick with the stink of diesel, fishmeal, and rust. Ham stood on the deck, the collar of his oilskin turned up against the damp, watching Tam secure the lines with a few efficient loops. No words passed between them. The silence was not companionable. It was the quiet of men who had seen the same thing and had drawn different conclusions.

Tam finished, wiped his hands on his trousers, and nodded towards the wheelhouse. “I’ll square the harbourmaster. You’re clear.”

“Where will you be?” Ham asked.

“Gone,” Tam said. It was not an evasion. It was a statement of operational fact. The boat was a tool. The job was done. The tool would be stored. The man would vanish. “You know how to reach me. If you need to.”

Ham hefted his kit bag, which contained little more than the clothes he stood in, his wallet, and the cold weight of the key. He stepped onto the slick concrete of the quay. The sound of his boots echoed in the cavernous quiet. He did not look back. He heard the low thrum of the Morna’s engine engaging, a slow retreat into the gloom.

He walked. The town was asleep, curtains drawn against the coming day. His mind was not on the platform, nor the crate, nor the key in his pocket. It was on the ledger. The one in his old office, with its whisky-stained pages. He had thought in columns of profit and loss, assets and liabilities. He had been a man who believed the world operated on a principle of measurable exchange. A debt was incurred, a debt was paid. A service rendered, a fee collected. It was a clean, comprehensible system.

The system he had seen was not clean. It did not keep books. It consumed. It consumed men like Grant, spitting out the bones. It consumed loyalty, twisting it into a weapon. It consumed truth and excreted a more useful fiction. It was a digestive tract, not a balance sheet. And he had just fed it a morsel.

He found an all-night café, a greasy cube of light in a terrace of shuttered shops. He took a booth by the window, the vinyl seat sighing under his weight. He ordered a black coffee and a full breakfast from a woman whose face was a monument to weary shifts. When it came, he ate methodically, tasting nothing, fuelling the machine. The radio played tinny pop music. The fryer hissed. The normalcy of it was a dissonant hum.

The key lay on the Formica table beside the brown sauce bottle. A simple steel fob, unmarked. It was a physical truth in a world of lies. It opened something. Something he had delivered. Therefore, the thing it opened was now in the possession of the enemy. The logic was inexorable, a syllogism written in cold steel.

His own role re-crystallised, sharp and ugly. He had not been a thief. He had been a deliveryman. A patsy with a specialised skillset. Grierson had not needed him to steal the crate. Grierson could have hired a team of bricks with crowbars for that. He had needed someone who could authenticate the goods, who could look at a manifest and know if the numbers had been fudged, who could smell a logistical lie. He had needed a quality assurance agent for a black operation. Ham had certified the product. His reputation, his pariah’s expertise, had been the seal on the package.

The coffee was bitter and thin. He drank it all.

He took a room in a tired bed-and-breakfast a street back from the front. The wallpaper was a faded pattern of violets, the carpet thin. He locked the door, placed a chair under the handle, and slept for four hours of dreamless, concrete oblivion. When he woke, the grey daylight was solid against the window. He showered in water that stuttered between scalding and cold, scrubbing the salt and the lingering sweet-metallic taint from his skin.

Dressed, he sat on the edge of the bed with a cheap pay-as-you-go phone. He dialled a number from memory, one he had not used in over a decade. It rang five times before it was answered with a grunt.

“It’s MacTavish.”

A long pause. The sound of a pipe being tapped out. “Christ. I heard you were dead. Or in a ditch. Which is it?”

“Neither. I need a name, Angus.”

“Do you now.” The voice was old gravel, the accent pure Govan. Angus ‘The Ledger’ McAllister was a retired accountant who had, for forty years, kept the books for organisations that did not officially exist. He was a human archive of illicit cashflows, a cartographer of hidden economies. He was also the only man Ham had ever met who understood numbers as a kind of moral philosophy.

“A company. Or a shell. Something with a taste for high-value, specialised industrial components. Logistics through the North Sea. Connections to the security apparatus. Deep pockets, but quiet. The kind that doesn’t need to turn a profit.”

Angus wheezed a laugh. “You’ve just described half the boardrooms in the country, son. You’ll need to be more specific.”

“They’re using decommissioned platforms as waypoints. They have their own boats. They have people inside the Crown Office and the NCA. They move things that don’t officially move.”

The line went quiet. Ham could hear the old man’s breath, the faint whistle in his lungs. “You’re talking about the private river,” Angus said finally, his voice dropping, all humour gone.

“What river?”

“The one that doesn’t flow on any map. The one that carries the things the government can’t be seen to buy, and the things corporations can’t be seen to sell. Defence, aerospace, energy tech. The grey market. But not the one run by spivs in Dubai. The domestic one. The polite one.”

“Who controls it?”

“Nobody controls it, Hamish. It’s a consortium. A understanding. A shared interest in deniability. You don’t find a name on a door. You find a pattern in the procurement. A whisper in a committee. A subsidiary of a subsidiary of a charity trust.” He paused. “You’ve stepped in it, haven’t you?”

“I delivered a package for them.”

Angus sighed, a sound of profound disappointment. “Then you’re already in the water. The only way out is to follow the current to the source. But I’ll tell you this for nothing—the source isn’t a man. It’s a vacuum. A need. A hole where accountability should be. You can’t put a key in that.”

“I have a key. A physical one.”

“Then it doesn’t open the room you think it does.” Another pause, longer. “There’s a firm. A consultancy. Ostensibly they advise on regulatory compliance for critical national infrastructure. They have an office in Edinburgh. Charlotte Square. The brass plate says ‘Peregrine Strategic Solutions.’ They’re cleaners. They don’t get their hands dirty. They just make sure the paperwork never points to the people who do.”

“Peregrine,” Ham repeated.

“Aye. Start there. And Hamish?”

“Yes?”

“Don’t call me again from this number. And if you’re going to start digging in that particular garden, for God’s sake don’t use a spade. Use a needle. Find one thread and pull. The whole tapestry might unravel. Or it might strangle you.”

The line went dead.

Ham sat for an hour, motionless, the phone in his hand. The violet wallpaper seemed to pulse. A consortium. A understanding. A system that had formalised corruption into a service industry. Peregrine Strategic Solutions. Cleaners.

He left the B&B, paid in cash, and walked to the railway station. He bought a ticket to Edinburgh with the last of the notes from the envelope Tam had given him. The train was half-empty. He took a seat by the window, watching the granite-grey landscape of Aberdeenshire give way to the softer green of Angus and Fife. The key was in his jacket pocket. He kept his hand around it, feeling its edges, its cold solidity.

Waverley Station was a cathedral of noise and rushing bodies. He merged with the flow, up the steps into the damp Edinburgh afternoon. The castle was a spectral outline in the haar. He walked through the New Town, the Georgian symmetry a stark contrast to the chaotic truth he carried. Charlotte Square was a perfect rectangle of ordered wealth, black iron railings guarding pristine gardens.

Number 17 was a four-storey townhouse of honeyed sandstone. The brass plate was discreet, polished to a dull gleam: Peregrine Strategic Solutions Ltd. No logo. No indication of business. The windows were clean, blank.

He did not go in. He crossed the square, sat on a bench facing the building. He took out the phone and searched the company. The website was a masterpiece of blandness: text about integrated risk mitigation and stakeholder alignment, stock photos of diverse, smiling teams in meetings. The ‘Our People’ page listed names and vague biographies: former civil servants, a retired brigadier, an ex-diplomat. The language was a fortress of nothingness.

He watched the door. A man in a dark overcoat went in. A woman with a leather folio came out. They looked like any other professionals in this city of lawyers and bankers. There was no stain on them.

This was the root. Not a gnarled, ugly thing thrusting from the earth, but a smooth, cultured lichen growing on the bedrock of power. It did not fight the system. It was the system, operating at a higher, quieter frequency. Grierson was a soldier. Grant was a blunt instrument. These people were the officers, directing battles from rooms that smelled of polished wood and espresso.

His phone buzzed. An unknown number.

He answered. “Yes.”

“The merchandise has been received.” It was Grierson’s voice, flat, devoid of static. A landline.

“I didn’t doubt it would be.”

“Your payment. The remainder. It’s been deposited. The account we discussed.”

Ham had almost forgotten about the money. The sum that was to be his fresh start. It felt like Monopoly money now. “Considerate.”

“We are considerate. We are also thorough. The transaction is complete. Your involvement is concluded.” A statement, with the force of an order.

“Is it?”

A beat of silence. “The key you retained. A souvenir?”

“A paperweight.”

“It opens nothing of value. A storage locker in a facility that no longer exists. Sentimental, perhaps. I’d discard it. Clean breaks are best.”

“I’ll take that under advisement.”

“Do,” Grierson said. The line went dead.

The threat was velvet-wrapped, but he felt its density. Your involvement is concluded. They were sweeping up. He was a loose end, but a known quantity. A businessman on the run, now paid. The easiest thing for him to do would be to take the money and disappear. They had calculated that. They had priced his silence.

He looked at the key in his palm. A storage locker in a facility that no longer exists. A lie. Grierson would not have mentioned it if it were worthless. The mention was an attempt to devalue it in his mind. To make him throw it away.

He stood from the bench. The afternoon was fading. He walked away from Charlotte Square, not towards the station, but into the Old Town, the labyrinth of closes and wynds. He needed a different kind of tool. Not a needle. A magnet.

He found a pub off the Royal Mile, a place of dark wood and darker whisky. He took a corner table, ordered a Lagavulin, and waited for the man he had texted from the train.

Fraser was twenty minutes late. He slid into the booth opposite Ham, his face pale, his eyes darting. He was a tech, a wizard Ham had used years before for discreet digital audits. He had the look of a man who lived on code and anxiety.

“You’re a ghost,” Fraser whispered. “They say you’re gone.”

“I need a ghost to find a ghost,” Ham said, pushing the key across the table. “This is physical. It opens something. Likely a secure container—a locker, a safe deposit box, a piece of industrial equipment. I need to know what system it belongs to. Manufacturer, model, typical users.”

Fraser picked up the key as if it were radioactive. He turned it over, examined the bow, the bit. “No markings. Could be anything. Generic.”

“It’s not generic. It’s specific. The lack of marking is the marking. It’s meant to be anonymous. That tells you it’s for a high-security system where keys are coded, not labelled. Think government. Think high-value commercial storage. Logistics.”

Fraser’s eyes glazed slightly, accessing internal catalogues. “There are a few. Chubb, Abloy, Mul-T-Lock. Could be a cruciform, a dimple… this is a standard pin tumbler, but the milling is…” He trailed off, squinting. “The shoulder. It’s been filed. Professionally. To alter the bitting code from the original.”

“Can you trace it?”

“Not trace. But… if I know the blank, I can guess the lock. If I guess the lock, I can guess who buys them in bulk.” He pulled out his phone, took several close-up photos of the key from different angles. “I’ll need to ask some people. Discreetly.”

“Discreet is the only way.”

Fraser pocketed the key. “I’ll need this. To show.”

Ham felt a surge of resistance, a primal urge to reclaim the one solid thing he had. He nodded. “Twenty-four hours.”

“It might cost.”

“Put it on my tab.”

Fraser left, melting into the pub’s gloom. Ham finished his whisky. The peat smoke taste was a memory of a different life, a different man. He was no longer that man. He was not a fugitive, nor a courier. He was an investigator with a single piece of evidence, standing outside the fortress.

He found a library before it closed. He used a public terminal to search property records, not for Peregrine, but for the holding companies that owned the building on Charlotte Square. It was a Russian doll of offshore entities, a trail that disappeared into the Caribbean after two clicks. He searched the names of the ‘Our People.’ The retired brigadier sat on the board of a charity funding veterans’ mental health. The ex-diplomat was a trustee of a museum. All clean. All connected.

This was the architecture of it. Not a conspiracy, but an ecosystem. A symbiotic network where influence was currency, and deniability was the highest good. To attack it was not to attack a king, but to attack the idea of the kingdom itself.

He walked back to the New Town as streetlights flickered on. He stood again in the shadow of the square, looking at Number 17. A light was on in a first-floor window. A silhouette moved, paused, looked out. It was impossible to tell if the figure was looking back at him. The distance was too great, the glass too reflective.

But he felt the gaze. The system had sensors. It was aware.

His phone buzzed again. Fraser.

“Already?” Ham answered.

“I got lucky. Or unlucky.” Fraser’s voice was tight. “The key. It’s for a BiLock NT series. Australian. High-security. Used mainly by… correctional services. And government archive facilities. But here’s the thing. The specific milling and the filing on the shoulder? It matches a batch procured five years ago by the Scottish Government. For a pilot project. Secure evidence storage for the Crown Office.”

Ham’s blood went still. “Evidence storage.”

“Aye. Not for paperwork. For physical exhibits. Contraband. Weapons. Sensitive materials from high-profile cases. The kind of stuff that needs to disappear but needs to be seen to be stored. The project was shelved. The locks were supposedly decommissioned, destroyed.”

“But they weren’t.”

“This one wasn’t. And the facility… the pilot was to be at a retrofitted site. A former Royal Mail sorting office in Leith. The project died, the building was sold to a property developer two years ago.”

“Who bought it?”

A rustle of papers. “A company called Caledonian Regeneration LLP. They’re… bloody hell.”

“What?”

“They’re a subsidiary. Of a subsidiary. Of a charitable trust. The same trust that one of your Peregrine directors chairs as an honorary treasurer.”

The thread. Thin, strong, humming with tension.

“The address,” Ham said.

Fraser gave it. A street in Leith, near the docks. “Ham, this is… this is beyond me. This is spook stuff. I’m out. The key’s in an envelope behind the bar where we met. Don’t call me again.”

The line died.

Ham stood in the gathering dark, the city’s elegant geometry now a grid of traps and triggers. The key did not open a crate. It opened a government evidence locker in a building that was not a government building, managed by a company that did not exist, for a system that consumed truth.

Grierson had said the key opened nothing of value. He was wrong. It opened a tomb. A tomb where the system buried its mistakes, its excesses, its inconvenient truths. And Ham had delivered a new body for it.

He had the location. He had the means of entry.

He also had the certain knowledge that it was a trap. Not a trap set for him, but a trap inherent in the design. A place where things were put so they could be forgotten. To go there was to become a thing that could be forgotten.

The hook was no longer in his hand. It was in his mind. A cold, logical imperative. To see. To know. To measure the true depth of the rot.

He turned his collar up against the evening chill and began to walk towards Leith. The door was open. The only way to close it was to walk through, and see what was inside waiting for him.

---

 Chapter 18

 CHAPTER 18

The building was a slab of shadow against the bruised plum of the Leith sky. It squatted between a shuttered chippy and a bonded warehouse, its Victorian red brick stained black by a century of coal-smoke and salt-spray. The windows were blind, covered from the inside with sheets of corrugated steel bolted into the frames. The grand archway, once the loading bay for mail vans, was sealed by a modern roller-shutter the colour of dull lead. A single security camera, its housing cracked and askew, gazed with a dead glass eye over a pavement littered with crisp packets and shattered brown glass.

Caledonian Regeneration LLP. A name for a tomb.

Ham stood across the street, in the deeper dark of a warehouse doorway. The chill here had a different quality. It was not the clean, sharp cold of the Highlands, which spoke of empty spaces and honest frost. This was a seepage, a damp exhalation from stone that had absorbed too many whispered secrets and bureaucratic sighs. The key in his pocket was a cold tooth against his thigh.

Grierson’s key. Meant for a crate. Meant for nothing of value.

He had walked from the New Town, a deliberate, measured pace, letting the certainty of the trap settle into his bones. It was not a sprung-jaw, teeth-and-wire thing. It was more elegant. More systemic. It was a function. You put the inconvenient thing in the designated place. The place was designed to consume attention, to erode will, to label and file and forget. To enter was to petition for your own erasure.

He crossed the street, his footsteps echoing too loud in the narrow canyon of the street. The roller-shutter had a pedestrian door set into it, a steel rectangle reinforced with rivets. The lock was a five-lever mortice, heavy-duty, but around its edges the paint was scratched, the metal shiny from repeated, careless engagement. A lock used by people who did not own the things they guarded.

The key from the bar—a simple Yale on a fob—slipped into his hand. Fraser’s final gift. It turned with a smooth, well-oiled clunk. The door opened inward a few inches, resisted by a security chain. Standard. He reached into the gap, felt for the chain’s slider. It was not engaged. The resistance was a wedge of wood, kicked under the door as a crude alarm. He nudged it aside with his toe, pushed the door open, and stepped into the tomb.

The air was a solid thing. It smelled of dust, damp plaster, and underneath, a faint, acrid tang of ozone and stale electronics. The vast space of the old sorting floor opened before him, a cathedral of dereliction. Strip lights hung dead from the high ceiling, their tubes ghostly in the faint ambient light bleeding from the street around the shutter’s edges. The floor was concrete, strewn with drifts of pigeon droppings and fallen plaster. In the centre of the space, like an altar, stood a construction.

It was a cage. A twenty-foot cube of heavy-gauge steel mesh, erected inside the cavernous room. Within it, rows of industrial shelving units stood in military lines, loaded not with mail sacks, but with grey, uniform storage boxes. Hundreds of them. Maybe thousands. Each was labelled with a white sticker, a barcode, and a string of alphanumeric code. A plastic walkway formed a perimeter inside the mesh, and the cage door was secured with a heavy padlock.

A government evidence locker. In a building that was not a government building.

His eyes adjusted. He saw the other details. The new electrical conduits snaking up the walls to a humming bank of server racks in a far corner, their indicator LEDs winking like malevolent fireflies. The motion sensor high on a pillar, its lens covered with a film of dust. And the door. On the far wall, where the supervisor’s offices would have been, a solid steel fire door, painted grey. It had a keycard reader beside it, glowing a soft, ready red.

He moved silently, his boots leaving prints in the dust. He approached the cage. The padlock was a serious piece of hardware, but the mesh itself… he reached out, pressed a thumb against the wire. It was cold, rigid. He followed the line of it down to where it was bolted to the floor. The concrete around the bolt was newer, a different shade. A retrofit. A stage set.

The trap was not the cage. The cage was the lure. The honest thing. The thing a man would look at and think: The secret is in there. I must get inside.

He turned his back on it. Walked towards the steel door.

There was no handle. Just the card reader and a small, brushed steel panel at waist height. He ran his fingers around the edge of the door. It was perfectly flush with the frame. No give. He stepped back, examining the frame. No visible hinges. It opened inward.

The keycard would be with a security guard. Or in an office upstairs. Or it did not exist at all.

He looked at the steel panel. It was featureless. He pressed it. Nothing. He pushed at the top, the bottom. It resisted, then gave a fraction at the lower right corner. He dug his fingernails into the hairline gap, pulled. The panel swung open on silent hinges, revealing a recess behind. Not a keycard slot. A keyhole.

A simple, brass, five-pin tumbler lock.

A laugh, a dry, soundless thing, formed in his chest. The elegance of it was almost beautiful. The brutal cage, the digital reader, the motion sensors—all theatre. A pantomime of security for the curious, the clumsy, the officially sanctioned. The real door, the one that mattered, opened with a physical key. A key for a man who understood that true power always, eventually, reduced itself to a physical metaphor. A key in a lock. A hand on a throat.

He took Grierson’s key from his pocket. The one that opened nothing of value. It was heavy, cold. The bow was ornate, a stylised thistle. He inserted it into the lock.

It turned. Not with a smooth clunk, but with a heavy, mechanical thunk that resonated deep within the wall. A magnetic seal disengaged with a sigh of released pressure. The door swung inward an inch, on its own weight.

Darkness waited beyond, smelling of chilled, recycled air and something else. Something organic, cloying. Like wet clay and disinfectant.

He pushed the door open and stepped through.

It closed behind him with a solid, final sound. The darkness was absolute. He stood still, listening. A low, almost sub-audible hum vibrated through the floor. Climate control. He took a step forward, hands out. His fingers brushed a wall, cold and smooth. Painted concrete.

A click. Then light.

Fluorescent tubes, shielded behind wire cages in the ceiling, flickered and buzzed to life in sequence, revealing a corridor. It was narrow, clinical, lit to a shadowless brightness. The walls were the same smooth, pale grey. The floor was grey vinyl, scuffed. Doors, also grey steel, were set at intervals along the left-hand wall, each with a numbered keypad and a small, thick glass viewport at eye level.

It was not a tomb. It was a archive. A cold store.

He moved to the first door. Number 001. The viewport was fogged around the edges. He wiped it with his thumb, looked in.

A room, ten feet by ten. A metal table, bolted to the floor. A chair. On the table, a grey storage box, identical to those in the cage outside. Its lid was off. Inside, he could see a jumble of objects: a woman’s handbag, leather, cracked with age; a man’s wristwatch; a ceramic mug with a chipped rim; a stack of letters tied with ribbon. Personal effects. The detritus of a life, curated, catalogued, and filed.

He moved to the next door. 002. A similar room. This box contained files, thick manila folders spilling typed pages. He could make out a header on a sheet: PROJECT NIGHTINGALE – TERMINATION AUDIT.

003. This box held VHS tapes, their labels handwritten: INTERVIEW SUBJECT DELTA – 12/11/1998.

He walked down the corridor, a cold fury tightening in his gut. This was the system’s gut. Its lower intestine. Where it compacted its waste into neat, numbered packages. Not destroyed. Preservation was more efficient than destruction. Destruction required energy, decision, risk. Preservation required only space, cold, and silence. You could always claim you had not destroyed the evidence. You had merely… filed it.

The corridor ended at another steel door. This one had no number, no keypad. Only a heavy lever handle. He pressed his ear to the cold metal. The hum was louder here, mixed with a new sound: a rhythmic, mechanical whisper, like the breath of a large, sleeping machine.

He depressed the lever. The door unsealed with a hiss.

The room beyond was larger, colder. The light was a bluish, antiseptic glow from LED strips. The source of the hum was a bank of industrial refrigeration units along the far wall, their grilles vibrating. But it was not the machines that held his gaze.

It was the tables.

Six stainless-steel mortuary tables, arranged in two rows of three. On each table lay a shape, covered by a heavy grey plastic sheet.

And at the far end of the room, under the starkest light, was a seventh table. This one was occupied. The sheet was pulled back to the waist, revealing the upper body of a man. He was shirtless, pale, his skin waxy under the blue light. His head was turned away, towards the wall.

Ham’s breath plumed in the chilled air. The organic smell was stronger here, barely contained by the chemical overlay. He walked forward, his footsteps silent on the non-slip floor. He passed the first covered table. The sheet outlined a human form, the contours unmistakable.

He reached the seventh table. He looked down at the man’s face.

It was Grierson.

Or what was left of him. The features were slack, bloodless. A fine tracery of purple lividity marked the side of his neck and shoulder where the blood had settled. His eyes were closed. There was no visible wound, no violence upon the flesh. He looked like a man who had simply, quietly, stopped. A man filed away.

The hook, the cold logical imperative, twisted in Ham’s mind. He had come to see the depth of the rot. He was standing in its frozen heart. This was not a trap set for him. It was the trap. The final destination for loose ends. Grierson had been processed. He was now a piece of data, stored in a cold room in Leith.

A soft, electronic chime sounded behind him.

He turned. A flat-panel screen on the wall by the door he had entered flickered to life. It showed a monochrome image from a camera high in the corner of this room. He saw himself from above, a broad, dark figure standing over the dead man on the slab. The image was crisp, merciless.

Text scrolled across the bottom of the screen, white on black.

SUBJECT: GRIERSON, ALASDAIR. ACCESSION  7741. STATUS: TERMINATED. CAUSE: SYSTEMIC COMPROMISE. AUDIT TRAIL: QUARANTINED.

Then, new text formed.

VISITOR DETECTED. SCANNING.

A thin red laser line emerged from a housing near the camera, sweeping down, painting a grid over his body. It passed over his face, his chest, his hands.

SCAN COMPLETE. IDENTIFICATION: MACTavish, HAMISH (HAM). ASSOCIATION: PRIMARY CONTACT, SUBJECT 7741. THREAT PROFILE: ELEVATED. INITIATING CONTAINMENT PROTOCOL.

The door through which he had entered emitted a solid clunk as heavy bolts shot home. The door at the opposite end of the room, previously unnoticed, did the same.

Containment. Not capture. Not interrogation. Containment.

He was now in the box.

He looked back at Grierson’s face. The man had tried to play the game. He had traded a key for his life. And the system had taken both, because the system could not tolerate an unbalanced equation. Ham had delivered the key. He had completed the transaction. His own value in the equation was now negative. A liability. A loose end in proximity to another loose end.

The fury crystallised into a cold, clear point. This was the corruption. Not the bribe, not the threat, not the backroom deal. This was the end product. The reduction of human beings to numbered subjects, their lives to audit trails, their deaths to ‘systemic compromise’. It was power so absolute it had dispensed with malice. It operated with the sterile efficiency of a refrigerator, humming in the dark, preserving what it needed to forget.

He walked to the wall near the bank of freezers. The conduits for the coolant lines were exposed, sheathed in white foam insulation. He followed them with his eye to where they entered the wall. There was an access panel, secured with four Phillips-head screws.

From his pocket, he took his keys. On the fob was a small, sturdy penknife he used for opening parcels. He opened the screwdriver blade. The screws were tight, but they turned. He worked methodically, his large hands precise. The panel came away, revealing a nest of pipes and electrical cables.

The screen on the wall updated.

UNAUTHORISED INTERVENTION DETECTED. SECURITY NOTIFIED.

He ignored it. He traced the main power conduit feeding the refrigeration units. It was thick, black, ribbed. He selected a smaller, insulated cable bundle beside it. Data lines. Control systems. He used the knife’s blade to score through the insulation, exposing the coloured wires within. He separated them. Then he reached into the power conduit’s junction box, found the main live feed. He yanked it free, a spark snapping in the dark cavity.

He touched the bare, live wire to the exposed data cables.

A flash. A pop. A shower of sparks from the junction box. The LED strips flickered, died, then came back on, but dimmer, running on emergency battery. The deep hum of the compressors choked, sputtered, and fell silent.

In the sudden, relative quiet, a new sound emerged. A rising, urgent alarm, whooping from speakers in the ceiling. Red emergency lights began to strobe.

ENVIRONMENTAL SYSTEMS FAILURE. TEMPERATURE DEVIATION. CONTAINMENT BREACH IMMINENT.

The bolts on the far door remained shut. The system’s protocol was clear: contain the breach. Let the environment handle the problem. Let the cold stabilise, or let the heat of a stranded body raise the temperature, triggering… what?

He went to the first covered table. He pulled back the grey sheet.

The woman underneath was young, her face peaceful in a way that only the dead achieve. She was dressed in a hospital gown. No identification.

He moved to the next table. An older man, his body frail.

He was not looking for identification. He was looking for a tool. On the fourth table, he found it. The subject was a large man. And on a small trolley beside the table, in a clear plastic bag labelled ‘EFFECTS’, was a leather belt. A heavy, brass buckle.

Ham took the belt. He wrapped the leather around his right hand twice, leaving the heavy buckle dangling. He walked to the door he had first entered, the one that led back to the numbered corridor. He studied the frame. The bolts had shot from the door into the steel frame. They would be thick, hardened.

He swung the belt. The brass buckle struck the doorjamb, right at the seam where the bolt met the frame. A sharp, ringing clang. A dent in the steel. He swung again. Again. Methodical, powerful blows. The alarm whooped. The red light strobed, turning the clinical room into a pulsing hell.

On the eighth blow, something gave. Not the bolt. The frame. The metal around the bolt housing, weakened by the constant, focused impact, deformed. A gap appeared, a quarter-inch between door and frame. He hooked the belt’s buckle into the gap, braced his foot against the wall, and pulled. Muscles corded in his neck and back. The metal groaned, then shrieked.

The bolt, still extended, tore free from its housing in the frame, taking a chunk of steel with it. The door swung open.

He was back in the bright corridor. The doors to the numbered rooms were blinking, small red LEDs above each keypad flashing in unison. He did not go back towards the cage and the main floor. They would be coming that way. Security. Or something else.

He ran the other way, down the corridor past the rooms, to where it ended at a T-junction. Left or right. Both identical corridors. He chose left. It ended at a heavy fire door. He hit the bar. It opened onto a concrete stairwell, lit by caged emergency lights. The alarm was louder here, echoing off the bare walls. He took the stairs two at a time, going up.

He emerged on the first-floor landing. Another corridor, this one with offices behind glass partitions. Dark. Empty. He moved past them, to a window at the far end. It was one of the windows he had seen from the outside, covered from within by a bolted steel sheet. He tried the office doors. Locked. He kicked one, next to the lock. The frame splintered, the door flew inwards.

The office was sparse. A desk, a chair, a filing cabinet. And on the wall, a fire axe behind glass.

He broke the glass, took the axe. He returned to the corridor window. He swung the axe. The blade bit deep into the steel sheeting with a terrific crash. He wrenched it free, swung again. On the third blow, he punched a gash in the metal. Cold night air rushed in. He hacked at the edges, bending the steel back until the hole was large enough to climb through.

He looked out. He was on the first floor, above the shuttered chippy. A drop of fifteen feet onto a pile of black rubbish bags. He threw the axe out, heard it thud on the bags. He climbed through the jagged hole, hung from the sill by his fingers, and let go.

The landing was soft, foul-smelling. He rolled, came up, grabbed the axe. He was in a narrow, foul alley. The whoop of the alarm was muffled now, coming from within the building. He could hear another sound, growing louder: the distant warble of sirens.

He walked to the alley’s mouth, peered out. The street was still empty. But blue lights strobed against the buildings at the far end, reflecting off the wet cobbles.

He turned and melted into the deeper dark of the alley network, moving away from the tomb, the axe still in his hand. The cold logical imperative had been satisfied. He had seen the rot. He had measured its depth. It was bottomless. It was institutional, automated, self-perpetuating.

And he had just kicked a hole in its side.

They would not file him now. He was no longer a loose end to be contained. He was a rupture. A systemic anomaly. They would have to come for him. Not with keys and cold rooms, but with force. The thought carried no fear. It carried a grim, clarifying weight.

The hook was no longer in his mind. It was in his hand. A fire axe, notched and cold. And the door was no longer open. He had torn it off its hinges. The only thing waiting for him now was the reckoning he had just summoned. It would come in the next chapter, and it would wear a uniform, or a suit, and it would speak in the calm, reasonable tones of a system defending its own existence. He needed to be ready. He needed a place to stand. He needed leverage.

He knew where to find it. Not in a ledger. Not in a secret file. In the one thing the system could not cold-store, could not reduce to an accession number. In the living, breathing, messy world outside its sterile walls. He had a name now. The charitable trust. The Peregrine director. The thread was no longer thin. It was a rope, and he had one end of it in his hands. All he had to do was pull, and see what corpse came swinging out of the dark.

---

 Chapter 19

 CHAPTER 19

The alley was a gash between two warehouses, slick with a patina of grease and rain. The air tasted of diesel and decayed fish. Ham walked, the axe a familiar, ugly weight in his grip. The sirens converged behind him, a chorus of mechanical distress. They were at the front door of the cold-storage facility now, the tomb he had just violated. They would find the broken door, the empty room, the shattered glass of the observation window. They would find the orderly, perhaps still unconscious, perhaps already spinning a tale of a single, deranged intruder. They would not find the ledger. That was ash in a stainless-steel sink.

He turned a corner, then another, moving by instinct through the labyrinth of the docks. The logical imperative had shifted. It was no longer about observation, about proving the rot to himself. The proof was cooling in his hand, its handle notched from biting into a door that was never meant to be opened. The action was taken. The system had been breached. Now came the reaction. It would be swift, and it would be total. They would not try to contain him. They would try to erase him.

He needed a hole to crawl into. Not a hiding place. A vantage point.

The rain began again, a fine, needling mist that beaded on the wool of his coat and the steel of the axe head. He saw a skip overflowing with sodden cardboard and splintered pallets. Without breaking stride, he swung the axe in a short, underhand arc and let it fly. It tumbled once, a dull gleam in the gloom, and buried itself with a soft thump amidst the waste. It was a tool that had served its purpose. Carrying it further was sentiment, and sentiment was a luxury his current circumstances could not afford.

He emerged onto a wider street, lit by the sickly orange glow of sodium lamps. A lone taxi was parked outside a shuttered newsagent, the driver hunched over a phone. Ham approached, tapped on the window. The driver jumped, his eyes wide as he took in the man before him: tall, broad, coat dark with more than rain, hands empty but held with a stillness that suggested they were rarely so.

“I need to go to the Highlands,” Ham said, his voice the low rumble of a banked fire.

The driver blinked. “The Highlands? Mate, that’s a two-hundred-quid fare. Minimum.”

Ham didn’t move. He didn’t repeat himself. He simply stood, letting the silence and the implication of his size settle over the transaction. He reached into his inner pocket, withdrew a sodden leather wallet. He extracted three hundred-pound notes, water-blurred but still legible. He held them up to the glass.

“Half now. Half when you’re five miles from the address I give you. You speak of it to anyone, you forget my face, you forget the fare. Or I find you and we have a different conversation.”

The driver looked at the money, then at Ham’s eyes, which held no threat, only a flat, factual promise. He swallowed, nodded, and unlocked the doors.

The taxi smelled of stale smoke and pine air-freshener. Ham gave an address not a home, but a place: a lay-by on the A82, north of Tyndrum, with a specific view of a specific mountain. It was a spot he knew from a different life, a place for a discreet meeting or a silent pickup. The driver keyed it into his sat-nav, the electronic voice a chirping absurdity in the tense cabin. They pulled away from the kerb.
